The Head of Knowledge: Knowing Your Lord
Hadith on the priority of fundamental knowledge over rare knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet described knowledge of the Lord (ma'rifat al-Rabb) as the 'head of knowledge' and the foundation before seeking other types of knowledge.

Core Idea
Mastering the knowledge of Allah comes before every other branch of learning.

The second hadith presents a man asking the Prophet for 'rare knowledge' (ghara'ib al-ilm). The Prophet redirects him to the 'head of knowledge' – knowing your Lord and preparing for death. This underscores that tawhid is the essential starting point, and only after mastering it can one delve into deeper or more specialized topics.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Notice the man's request for 'rare types of knowledge' and the Prophet's response. Identify what the Prophet calls the 'head of knowledge'.
The man thought rare knowledge was something advanced, but the Prophet went to the basics.
2
Understand the Prophet's questions
The Prophet asks: 'Do you know the Lord?' and 'Do you know death?' These are not optional; they are foundational. Reflect on how these two questions cover the essence of tawhid and accountability.
Knowing death and preparing for it is also part of beneficial knowledge.
3
Apply the lesson
Consider how this hadith prioritizes learning tawhid over all other Islamic sciences. Before studying fiqh, hadith, or spirituality, the first priority is to know Allah properly.
This is a reminder to many students today who jump into specialized topics without solidifying their aqidah.

1 Some of the traditions and reports indicating the honor of the science of tawhid: Among what indicates the honor of this science and its great merit is what has been narrated that it was said: O Messenger of God, which deed is most virtuous? He said: ‘Knowledge of God, Almighty and Majestic.’ It was said: O Messenger of God, we ask you about action, and you answer about knowledge?! He said: ‘Indeed, a little action benefits with knowledge of God, and indeed, much action does not benefit with ignorance of God.’ (Reported by Ibn Abd al-Barr in Jami' Bayan al-Ilm wa Fadlihi, 214, from the hadith of our master Anas, may God be pleased with him, and by al-Quda'i in Musnad al-Shihab, 1015, from the hadith of our master Ibn Mas'ud, may God be pleased with him.) And it has been narrated from him, may God bless him and grant him peace, that a man came to him and said: O Prophet of God, teach me from the rare types of knowledge. He said: ‘What have you done with the head of knowledge that you seek its rarities?’ He said: What is the head of knowledge, O Prophet of God? He said: ‘Do you know the Lord?’ He said: Yes. He said: ‘What have you done regarding His right upon you?’ He said: As God wills. He said: ‘Do you know death?’ He said: Yes. He said: ‘What have you prepared for it?’ He said: As God wills. He said: ‘Go and master what is here, and when you have mastered it, then come and I will teach you from the rare types of knowledge.’ (Reported by al-Jawhari in Musnad al-Muwatta, 10, and by Ibn Abd al-Barr in Jami' Bayan al-Ilm wa Fadlihi, 1222, from the hadith of Abdullah ibn al-Musawwar, may God have mercy on him, as a mursal report.) And it has been narrated in the report that God Almighty revealed to David, peace be upon him: O David, learn beneficial knowledge. He said: O Lord, what is beneficial knowledge? He said: That you know My majesty, My greatness, My pride, and the perfection of My power over everything. For this is the beneficial knowledge that brings you near to Me. (Mentioned by the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali in Minhaj al-Abidin, p. 51, and he advised, saying: Let the seeking be seeking of understanding, not seeking of transmission. And know that the danger is great. Whoever seeks knowledge to turn people's faces towards him, to sit with princes, to boast to peers, and to hunt for rulers... his trade is in ruin and his bargain is a loss.)) And in the Mighty Quran: ‘Only those among His servants who are knowledgeable fear God’ [Fatir: 28].

It begins with a subheading indicating that these are some of the traditions (athar) and reports (akhbar) indicating the honor of this science. This establishes the priority of knowledge of God over all actions. The second hadith involves a man asking the Prophet to teach him rare knowledge; the Prophet first asks about the 'head of knowledge' — which is knowing the Lord, and also knowing death and preparing for it. Together, these texts argue for the supreme value and necessity of learning tawhid.

What did the Prophet identify as the 'head of knowledge'?
A) Knowledge of the Quran and Hadith
B) Knowledge of the Lord and knowledge of death
C) Knowledge of the unseen
D) Knowledge of the Prophet's biography

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the Prophet asked, 'What have you done regarding His right upon you?' Stop and think: What specific right of Allah have you been neglecting today? What is one concrete action you will take to fulfill it before this day ends?
?
The Prophet asked about preparation for death. Are you truly prepared to meet your Lord? If not, what is one real step you will take today—not tomorrow—to begin that preparation?
?
You now know that knowing your Lord is the head of knowledge. Does your daily life reflect that priority? If your heart and time are filled with other pursuits, what will you remove to make room for this foundational knowledge?

Beneficial Knowledge: Knowing Allah's Attributes
Divine revelation and Quranic verse on beneficial knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that beneficial knowledge is knowledge of Allah's majesty, greatness, and power, and that such knowledge leads to true fear of Him.

Core Idea
Knowledge that draws you closer to Allah is knowledge of His attributes and reverential fear.

The next proof is a divine revelation to Prophet David (peace be upon him) defining 'beneficial knowledge' as knowledge of Allah's majesty, greatness, and perfect power. This is followed by a Quranic verse stating that only the knowledgeable truly fear Allah. Together, they ground the study of tawhid in both divine command and scriptural authority, showing that this knowledge is not merely theoretical but leads to spiritual transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the revelation to Dawud
Read how Allah defines beneficial knowledge: knowing His jalal (majesty), azamah (greatness), kibrīya' (pride), and perfect power. Notice the emphasis on knowing Allah's attributes.
These attributes are central to tawhid.
2
Reflect on the purpose of this knowledge
The revelation says this knowledge 'brings you near to Me.' Consider how knowledge of Allah's greatness instills humility and draws the heart closer to Him.
Knowledge without implementation is not truly beneficial.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
Read the verse from Surah Fatir: 'Only those among His servants who are knowledgeable fear Allah.' This shows that true knowledge produces khashyah (reverential fear), which is the fruit of ilm al-tawhid.
Fear here is not terror but awe and reverence.

?
If you were to stand before Allah today, could you honestly say that your knowledge of Him has made you a different person — more fearful, more loving, more obedient? If not, what is the barrier in your heart that you need to remove?

The Head of Knowledge is Knowledge of Allah
The Prophet and divine revelation define the head and benefit of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge of Allah is the foundation and most beneficial knowledge, as taught by the Prophet and revealed to Prophet Dawud.

Core Idea
Knowing Allah is the head of all knowledge.

This lesson presents two sacred narrations that establish the primacy of knowing Allah. A man asked the Prophet for esoteric knowledge, but the Prophet redirected him to the basics: first know your Lord and prepare for death. Then, a divine revelation to Dawud defines beneficial knowledge as knowledge of Allah's majesty and power. Together, they teach that the most important knowledge is ma'rifat Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the two narrations
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: the first is a dialogue with the Prophet, the second is a divine revelation to Dawud.
The passage combines a prophetic hadith and a holy hadith (hadith qudsi).
2
Identify the 'head of knowledge'
Find where the Prophet defines the 'head of knowledge' (ra's al-'ilm). What does he point to?
He asks 'Have you known the Lord?' – this shows the head of knowledge is knowing Allah.
3
Define beneficial knowledge
In the second narration, what does Allah tell Dawud is 'beneficial knowledge' (al-'ilm al-nafi')? List the attributes mentioned.
Beneficial knowledge is knowing Allah's majesty, greatness, pride, and complete power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وروي عنه صلى الله عليه وسلم: أنه أتاه رجل فقال له: يا نبي الله، علمني من غرائب العلم، فقال: «ما فعلت في رأس العلم حتى تطلب غرائبه؟»، قال: وما رأس العلم يا نبي الله؟ قال: «أعرفت الرب؟»، قال: نعم، قال: «فما فعلت في حقه عليك؟»، قال: ما شاء الله، قال: «أعرفت الموت؟»، قال: نعم، قال: «فما أعددت له؟»، قال: ما شاء الله، فقال: «انطلق وأحكم ما ها هنا، فإذا أحكمت فتعال أعلمك من غرائب العلم». وروي في الخبر: أن الله تعالى أوحى إلى داود عليه السلام: يا داود، تعلم العلم النافع، قال: يا رب، وما العلم النافع؟ قال: أن تعرف جلالي وعظمتي وكبريائي وكمال قدرتي على كل شيء، فإن هذا هو العلم النافع الذي يقربك إلي.
And it was narrated from him (peace be upon him) that a man came to him and said: O Prophet of Allah, teach me some of the strange knowledge. He said: 'What have you done with the head of knowledge that you seek its strange aspects?' He said: What is the head of knowledge, O Prophet of Allah? He said: 'Have you known the Lord?' He said: Yes. He said: 'Then what have you done regarding His right upon you?' He said: Whatever Allah wills. He said: 'Have you known death?' He said: Yes. He said: 'Then what have you prepared for it?' He said: Whatever Allah wills. He said: 'Go and master what is here, and when you have mastered it, then come and I will teach you from the strange knowledge.' And it was narrated in the report that Allah Almighty revealed to Dawud (peace be upon him): O Dawud, learn beneficial knowledge. He said: O my Lord, what is beneficial knowledge? He said: That you know My majesty, greatness, pride, and complete power over everything, for this is the beneficial knowledge that brings you near to Me.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a narration of two hadiths: the first from the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the second from a divine revelation to Prophet Dawud (peace be upon him). The first hadith establishes that knowing Allah ('the Lord') is the 'head of knowledge' (ra's al-'ilm), meaning the foundation and most important part. A man asked for 'strange' or esoteric knowledge, but the Prophet redirected him to the fundamentals: first, ensure you know your Lord and fulfill His rights, and know death and prepare for it. Only after mastering these basics should one seek deeper knowledge. The second hadith identifies beneficial knowledge (al-'ilm al-nafi') as knowledge of Allah's majesty, greatness, pride, and complete power. This knowledge is what brings a person close to Allah. Together, these narrations emphasize that the primary and most beneficial knowledge is knowledge of Allah and His attributes.

Key Terms
رأس العلم (ra's al-'ilm): head of knowledge
غرائب العلم (ghara'ib al-'ilm): strange/esoteric knowledge
العلم النافع (al-'ilm al-nafi'): beneficial knowledge

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that esoteric or miraculous knowledge is the highest form of knowledge.
Correction: The Prophet teaches that foundational knowledge of Allah (ma'rifat Allah) is the 'head of knowledge' and the most important, while esoteric knowledge comes after mastering the basics.

Checkpoint
What does the Prophet identify as the 'head of knowledge' (ra's al-'ilm)?
A) The science of the Hereafter
B) Knowledge of the Lord (ma'rifat al-Rabb)
C) Knowledge of death
D) Knowledge of beneficial trades

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of Allah is the head and most beneficial knowledge; it is the foundation upon which all other knowledge should be built.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that knowledge of Allah is the head of all knowledge. But what have you actually done today to increase that knowledge? Have you studied His names and attributes, or have you been distracted by other types of knowledge? How will you change your priorities starting now?
?
The Prophet asked the man: 'What have you done regarding His right upon you?' Be honest: when you say you believe in Allah, what rights of His have you been neglecting? What specific act of worship or obedience will you commit to tomorrow to start fulfilling that right?
?
You know you will die, and you claim to have prepared. But the man in the hadith said 'Whatever Allah wills' — a vague answer. The Prophet sent him back to master the fundamentals. What does your preparation for death look like? Is it a vague hope, or is it concrete action? Write down one thing you will do this week to prepare for your meeting with Allah.

The Knowledge That Necessitates Khashyah and Ibrahim's Proof
Sanusi defines knowledge that necessitates khashyah and cites Ibrahim's proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the knowledge which necessarily produces fear of Allah is specifically knowledge of Allah Himself, and that rational theology (kalam) is the path of the prophets and the Imams of Sunnah.

Core Idea
True khashyah comes only from knowing Allah through rational proofs.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a key point: not all religious knowledge produces fear of Allah. The knowledge that does is knowledge of Allah's essence and attributes. He then anchors this in the Qur'anic story of Prophet Ibrahim, whose rational argument for creation's temporality was honored by Allah. This passage establishes that rational theology (kalam) is not only legitimate but the very means to the highest spiritual stations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses Qur'anic evidence to support his claim.
2
Identify the specific knowledge that necessitates khashyah
The passage begins by defining which knowledge entails khashyah. Find the exact phrase and note that it is exclusively knowledge of Allah.
3
Understand Ibrahim's proof
The passage summarizes Ibrahim's argument: the world undergoes changes, so it must have a creator who is unchanging. See how this rational proof is presented as a divine argument.
4
Observe the divine command and its followership
Allah commands following Ibrahim's way (millah). The Imams of Sunnah are praised for doing so. Reflect on the connection between rational theology and high spiritual rank.
This step ties the lesson together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّ الْمُرَادَ بِالْعِلْمِ الَّذِي يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْخَشْيَةَ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الْعِلْمُ بِاللهِ تَعَالَى . وَقَالَ تَعَالَى بَعْدَ اسْتِدْلَالِ خَلِيلِهِ إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَى نَبِيِّنَا وَعَلَيْهِ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ عَلَى حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ بِمُلَازَمَتِهِ التَّغَيُّرَاتِ ، وَأَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ لِجَمِيعِهِ مِنْ مُخْتَرِعٍ وَمُدَبِّرٍ لَهُ لَا يَتَغَيَّرُ وَلَا تَحُلُّ بِهِ الْحَوَادِثُ : ﴿ وَتِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا آتَيْنَهَا إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَى قَوْمِهِ نَرْفَعُ دَرَجَاتٍ مَّن نَّشَاءُ ﴾ [الأنعام : ٨٣] ، فَأَضَافَ تَعَالَى تِلْكَ الْحُجَّةَ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ إِضَافَةَ تَشْرِيفٍ ، وَحَكَمَ بِرَفْعِ دَرَجَاتِ مَنْ فَتَحَ لَهُ فِي مَعْرِفَةِ الْحَقِّ بِبَرَاهِينِهِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ ، وَقَدْ أَمَرَنَا جَلَّ وَعَلَا بِالِاقْتِدَاءِ بِخَلِيلِهِ إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَيْهِ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : ( مِلَّةَ أَبِيكُمْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ ) [الحج : ٧] ، وَقَالَ : ﴿ ثُمَّ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَيْكَ أَنِ اتَّبِعْ مِلَّةَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ حَنِيفًا ﴾ [النحل : ١٢٣] . وَلَا شَكَّ أَنَّ أَئِمَّةَ السُّنَّةِ رَضِيَ اللهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُمْ هُمُ الَّذِينَ اقْتَدَوْا بِهَذَا الْأَمْرِ ، وَفَازُوا بِرَفْعِ الدَّرَجَاتِ وَنَيْلِ أَعْلَى الْمَرَاتِبِ عِنْدَ اللهِ تَعَالَى جَعَلَنَا اللهُ مِنْهُمْ دُنْيَا وَأُخْرَى
And it is known that the intended meaning of the knowledge that entails khashyah (fear of Allah) is only knowledge of Allah, the Exalted. And Allah the Exalted said after the reasoning of His intimate friend Ibrahim, may salat and peace be upon our Prophet and upon him, for the temporality of the world by its being subject to changes, and that all of it must have an inventor and manager who does not change and in whom events do not occur: {And that was Our argument which We gave to Ibrahim against his people. We raise in degrees whom We will} [al-An'am 6:83]. So Allah the Exalted attributed that argument to Himself as an attribution of honor, and decreed the raising of degrees for whomever He opens the way to knowledge of the truth through His rational proofs. And He, Glorified and Exalted, commanded us to follow His intimate friend Ibrahim, peace and salat be upon him, in His saying: (The religion of your father Ibrahim) [al-Hajj 22:7], and He said: {Then We inspired you to follow the religion of Ibrahim, upright} [an-Nahl 16:123]. And there is no doubt that the Imams of the Sunnah, may Allah be pleased with them, are the ones who followed this command, and succeeded in attaining elevation of degrees and reaching the highest stations with Allah the Exalted. May Allah make us among them in this world and the next.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the superiority of knowledge of Allah (ilm al-kalam) over other forms of knowledge. It begins by stating the well-known point that the knowledge which necessarily produces khashyah (fear of Allah) is specifically knowledge of Allah Himself—not knowledge of branches of law or other subjects. Then it cites Qur'anic proof: after Ibrahim presented his rational argument for the temporality of the world (based on the fact that the world constantly changes, so it must have a creator who is unchanging and free from events), Allah declared that this was His own argument given to Ibrahim, and that He raises in degrees those who are opened to recognizing the truth through rational proofs. Then Allah commanded the Prophet to follow Ibrahim's way (millah). The passage concludes by asserting that the Imams of the Sunni community are precisely those who followed this path, thus attaining the highest ranks from Allah. The prayer at the end expresses the hope that the reader be included among them. The overall argument is that rational theology (kalam) is not only permitted but the means to the highest spiritual station.

علم
خشية
حجة عقلية
ملة إبراهيم
براهين عقلية
أئمة السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any religious knowledge produces khashyah.
Correction: The passage specifies that only knowledge of Allah (His essence and attributes) necessarily entails khashyah, not knowledge of law or other subjects.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what kind of knowledge necessarily leads to khashyah?
A) Knowledge of Islamic law
B) Knowledge of Allah the Exalted
C) Knowledge of creation
D) Knowledge of the hereafter

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of Allah through rational proofs is the path of the prophets and the Imams of Sunnah, and it is the knowledge that truly produces fear of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do I truly fear Allah in a way that changes my daily choices, or is my 'fear' just a comfortable feeling that costs me nothing? Have I ever sat down to rationally know who Allah is, or do I rely on inherited beliefs without personal conviction?
?
When I recite the story of Ibrahim, do I see it as something that happened long ago, or do I feel the urgency to follow his method of thinking—questioning, reasoning, and arriving at certainty? What would it take for me to own my faith like he did?
?
The Imams of the Sunnah were raised in degrees because they followed Ibrahim's rational proofs. Am I content with my current level of knowledge, or do I long to be among those whom Allah opens the way to truth? What am I willing to sacrifice to attain that elevated station?

Why Knowing Allah Excels Knowing His Rulings
Sanusi argues for the superiority of those who know Allah's attributes over legal scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the knowers of Allah (al-ʿārifūn bi-llāh) are considered superior to legal scholars, based on the nobility of the known object and the fruits of that knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge is honored by the honor of its object; knowing Allah is the highest knowledge.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary presents a powerful argument for the supremacy of those who study the divine attributes over those who study legal rulings. Citing authorities like al-Ghazali and al-Qushayri, it culminates in 'Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam's reasoning that the nobility of knowledge derives from its subject matter. As you read, notice how the argument moves from authority to logical principle to practical fruit.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in before analyzing details.
The passage begins with a citation and ends with a clear conclusion.
2
Identify the Authorities Cited
Notice that Sanusi first reports statements from al-Ghazali and al-Qushayri about the precedence of saints and knowers of Allah. This sets the stage for the main argument.
al-Ghazali is quoted as saying the secrets of divinity are only for prophets and saints.
3
Grasp the Core Principle
Focus on 'Izz al-Din's statement: 'knowledge is honored by the honor of the known.' This is the key logical premise. Ask yourself: why does the object of knowledge determine its value?
The object here is Allah, the most honorable being.
4
Apply the Principle to Two Kinds of Knowledge
Compare knowledge of Allah's attributes (necessary, impossible) with knowledge of His rulings (furūʿ and uṣūl). According to the principle, the former is more honorable because its object is more noble.
This is the direct application of the principle.
5
Consider the Practical Fruits
Notice how the passage describes the fruits: hope from knowing mercy, fear from knowing punishment, and that fear produces obedience. See how this shows the practical superiority of this knowledge.
The fruits are not just intellectual but transform behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ تَفْضِيلُ الْعَارِفِينَ بِالْأُصُولِ عَلَى الْعَالِمِينَ بِالْفُرُوعِ ] وَقَدْ وَقَعَ فِي كَلَامِ الْغَزَالِيِّ : أَنَّ أَسْرَارَ الْإِلَهِيَّاتِ لَا يَسْتَقِلُّ بِهَا وَبِالْوُقُوفِ عَلَى طُرُقِ بَعْضِهَا إِلَّا الْأَنْبِيَاءُ وَالْأَوْلِيَاءُ . وَوَقَعَ فِي كَلَامِهِ تَقْدِيمُ الْأَوْلِيَاءِ عَلَى الْعُلَمَاءِ فِي مَعْرِفَةِ اللهِ تَعَالَى بَعْدَ الْأَنْبِيَاءِ . وَوَقَعَ نَحْوُهُ فِي رِسَالَةِ الْقُشَيْرِيِّ . وَسُئِلَ عَنْ ذَلِكَ عِزُّ الدِّينِ بْنُ عَبْدِ السَّلَامِ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى ، فَقَالَ : ( أَمَّا تَفْضِيلُ الْعَارِفِينَ بِاللهِ تَعَالَى عَلَى الْعَارِفِينَ بِأَحْكَامِ اللهِ تَعَالَى .. فَقَوْلُ الْأَسْتَاذِ وَالْغَزَالِيِّ فِيهِ مُتَّفَقٌ ; لِأَنَّ الْعَارِفِينَ بِمَا يَجِبُ للهِ تَعَالَى مِنْ أَوْصَافِ الْكَمَالِ ، وَبِمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ مِنَ النُّقْصَانِ.. أَفْضَلُ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْفُرُوعِ وَالْأُصُولِ ; لِأَنَّ الْعِلْمَ يَشْرُفُ بِشَرَفِ الْمَعْلُومِ ، وَالْعِلْمُ بِاللهِ تَعَالَى وَصِفَاتِهِ أَشْرَفُ ; لِأَنَّ مَعْلُومَهُ أَشْرَفُ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ ، وَثَمَرَتَهُ أَفْضَلُ الثَّمَرَاتِ . فَمَنْ عَرَفَ سَعَةَ رَحْمَةِ اللهِ تَعَالَى أَثْمَرَتْ مَعْرِفَتُهُ سَعَةَ الرَّجَاءِ ، وَمَنْ عَرَفَ شِدَّةَ النِّقْمَةِ أَثْمَرَتْ مَعْرِفَتُهُ شِدَّةَ الْخَوْفِ ، وَأَثْمَرَ خَوْفُهُ الْكَفَّ عَنِ الْفُسُوقِ وَالْعِصْيَانِ ، مَعَ الْبُكَاءِ وَالْأَحْزَانِ ، وَالْوَرَعِ وَالْإِذْعَانِ .
[The preference of those who know the fundamentals over those who know the branches] And it occurred in the speech of al-Ghazali: that the secrets of the divine matters are not independently known, nor the access to some of their ways, except by prophets and saints. And it occurred in his speech: the precedence of saints over scholars in knowledge of Allah the Exalted after the prophets. And similar occurred in al-Qushayri's Treatise. And 'Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam, may Allah have mercy on him, was asked about that, so he said: 'As for preferring those who know Allah the Exalted over those who know Allah's rulings... the opinion of the master and al-Ghazali agrees on it; because those who know what is necessary for Allah of attributes of perfection, and what is impossible for Him of deficiency... are better than those of the branches and the fundamentals; because knowledge is honored by the honor of the known, and knowledge of Allah the Exalted and His attributes is more honorable; because its known object is the most honorable of known things, and its fruit is the best of fruits. So whoever knows the extent of Allah's mercy, his knowledge produces breadth of hope; and whoever knows the severity of punishment, his knowledge produces intensity of fear; and his fear produces restraint from immorality and disobedience, along with weeping, sorrows, scrupulousness, and submission.'
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument for the superiority of those who know Allah's essence and attributes (the 'arifeen bi'llah) over those who know only the branches (fur') or even the fundamentals (usul) of law. It begins by citing al-Ghazali's statement that only prophets and saints can independently access the secrets of divine matters, and that saints are ranked above scholars in knowledge of Allah after the prophets. It also cites al-Qushayri's similar view. Then it presents the response of 'Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam, who affirms that the knowers of Allah are unanimously recognized as superior. His reason has two parts: (1) knowledge derives its honor from the nobility of its object; since the object here is Allah Himself—who possesses all perfection and is free from deficiency—that knowledge is the most noble. (2) The fruit of this knowledge is the best: knowledge of Allah's mercy produces hope, knowledge of His punishment produces fear, and that fear leads to practical obedience (restraint from sin, weeping, scrupulousness, and submission). Thus the passage justifies the primacy of kalam over other sciences.

Key Terms
تفضيل
عارف
غزالي
قديري
عز الدين بن عبد السلام
شرف المعلوم
ثمرة العلم
رجاء
خوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many believe that scholars of Islamic law (fuqahāʾ) are the highest rank among Muslims, surpassing those who merely study theology.
Correction: This passage argues that those who know Allah's attributes (the ʿārifūn) are superior because their knowledge has a nobler object and produces the best fruits, including hope, fear, and righteous action.

Checkpoint
Why does 'Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam consider those who know Allah superior to those who know His rulings?
A) Because they are more pious
B) Because knowledge is honored by the honor of its object
C) Because they perform more acts of worship
D) Because they have more miracles

Key Takeaway
The honor of knowledge comes from its object; therefore, knowledge of Allah's attributes is the most honorable, producing the best fruits in the believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You spend hours learning fiqh and usul, but how much time do you dedicate to directly knowing Allah's attributes—His perfection, His mercy, His severity? If you were to die today, would your knowledge of Allah produce hope or fear in your heart?
?
When you sin, do you feel a genuine fear of Allah's punishment that restrains you, or do you rely on knowing the legal ruling without internalizing the majesty of the One you disobey? What would it take for your knowledge to transform into weeping and submission?
?
If knowledge is honored by its object, what does the content of your study say about your priorities? Are you seeking the most honorable knowledge, or are you content with lesser branches? How will you change your pursuit starting today?

The Sacred Foundation of Tawhid
States that only the spiritually blind disparage the science of Tawhid, as it is based on the Quran and Sunnah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that disparaging the science of Tawhid is equivalent to disparaging the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus of the Muslim community.

Core Idea
To attack the science of Tawhid is to attack revelation itself.

Sanusi begins by addressing a common objection: that the science of kalam is a human innovation and therefore open to criticism. He declares that only those whose hearts are blinded by Allah would denigrate this science, because it is derived directly from the Quran and Sunnah. The passage establishes the sacred foundation of Tawhid as a discipline and warns that rejecting it means rejecting the core sources of Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the strong language used by Sanusi.
Notice the repetition of 'blinding'.
2
Understand the logical progression
Identify the three parts: (1) who disparages, (2) what that implies, (3) the consequence.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author equates criticism of tawhid with criticism of the primary sources. How does this defend the science?
This is a defense of kalam as a legitimate Islamic discipline.

Commentary
This is a short but powerful statement. The author declares that any attack on the science of tawhid (ilm al-tawhid) is essentially an attack on the Quran and Sunnah and the consensus of the ummah, because this science is derived from them. He then consoles that whoever is blinded by Allah cannot be guided by you.

Critique of Neglecting Theology
A scholar criticizes those who only study worldly sciences while ignoring fundamentals of religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of those who focus solely on worldly sciences while neglecting the fundamentals of theology, as illustrated by Shaykh 'Abd al-Jalil's vivid depiction of their ignorance and arrogance.

Core Idea
Worldly knowledge without theology leaves one intellectually impotent and spiritually bankrupt.

Shaykh 'Abd al-Jalil presents a powerful critique of people who dedicate themselves exclusively to worldly sciences such as grammar and arithmetic, hoping to gain material benefit and prestige. He shows that when faced with essential theological questions, they are reduced to silence and disgrace. This passage serves as a warning about the consequences of neglecting the core obligations of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the author's vivid language and critique.
Pay attention to the strong imagery used.
2
Identify the two groups
Notice the contrast between those who pursue worldly sciences for gain and the one who is challenged with theological questions.
3
Understand the questions asked
The passage lists three key theological questions: what is the first obligation, when does obligation begin, and what is the evidence for one's beliefs?
These questions are fundamental to Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the imagery
The author uses striking comparisons (silent as a fish, stuck as a bird) to emphasize the scholar's helplessness.
5
Consider the lesson
Think about the importance of prioritizing theology over worldly pursuits and the spiritual consequences of neglect.

قال الشيخ أبو القاسم عبد الجليل في ( عقيدته (۱) : ( اعلم : أنَّ كثيراً من الناس لا يشتغلون إلا بعلم النحو والحساب ، وإصلاح اللفظ وإجادة الكتاب ؛ لما يرجونَ مِنْ كون ذلك لهم بضاعةً وحرفةً يُعوّلونَ عليها وصناعةً ، فتراهم يَجُرُّونَ أذيالهم مِنْ خُيلائِهم ، ويذهبونَ متعاظمينَ في غُلَوائِهم ، يلحظونَ الناسَ بعين الاحتقار ، ويرمقونهم بمقلة الاستصغار ، فإذا قيلَ لهُ : ما أَوَّلُ الواجباتِ ؟ ومتى يجب التكليفُ على الإنسانِ ؟ وما الدليلُ على صحة ما إليه تذهبُ ، وفسادِ ما عنه ترغب ؟ .. بقي أسكتَ مِنْ سمكة ، وأشدَّ وُحُولاً مِنْ طائرٍ في شبكة (٢) ، وصغرَ في همَّتِهِ ما كانَ كبيراً ، وذلَّ في نفسِهِ ما كانَ عزيزاً خطيراً ، ولَبسَ ثوبَ استكانة ، وتسربل بسربال مهانة ، فيا لها مِنْ مصيبة ما أعظمها عليهِ ! وداهية ما أكبرها لديهِ ! ) انتهى (۱) قال العلامة المؤرخ ابن الأبار البلنسي في التكملة لكتاب الصلة » : ( عبد الجليل بن أبي بكر الربعي القروي ، يعرف بالديباجي وبابن الصابوني ، ويكنى أبا القاسم ، يروي عن أبي عمران الفاسي ، وأبي القاسم الخرقي وأبي عبد الله الأزدي صاحب القاضي أبي بكر بن الطيب ، وكان عالماً بالأصول ، مدرساً لها ، وله فيها تصانيف ، منها كتاب « المستوعب في أصول الفقه » ، ومنها كتاب « الانتصار » ، اختصره من كتاب « الانتصار » لأبي بكر بن الطيب ، وله ( رسالة في الاعتقادات » ) ، فهو من أعيان النصف الثاني من القرن الخامس (۲) قوله : ( وحولاً ) من الوَحَل ؛ وهو الطين الرقيق ، يقال : وَحِلَت الدابة في الطين ؛ وقعت فيه فلم تستطع عنه قياماً.
Shaykh Abu al-Qasim 'Abd al-Jalil said in his 'Creed': 'Know that many people occupy themselves only with the science of grammar and arithmetic, correcting pronunciation and perfecting writing, because they hope that this will be for them a commodity and a craft to rely upon and a trade. So you see them dragging their tails in arrogance, and walking in self-conceit in their excess, glancing at people with contempt, and looking at them with disdain. If it is said to him: "What is the first of the obligations? When does obligation become incumbent upon a person? What is the evidence for the correctness of what you hold and the corruption of what you reject?" ... he remains more silent than a fish, and more stuck than a bird in a net (2). And what was great in his aspiration becomes small, and what was noble and valuable in his own eyes becomes low. He puts on the garment of humility and cloaks himself in the cloak of disgrace. What a calamity, how great it is upon him! What a disaster, how great it is before him!' End. (1) The historian scholar Ibn al-Abbar al-Balansi said in the supplement to the book 'al-Sila': 'Abd al-Jalil ibn Abi Bakr al-Rib'i al-Qarawi, known as al-Dibaji and Ibn al-Sabuni, and his kunya is Abu al-Qasim. He narrated from Abu 'Imran al-Fasi, Abu al-Qasim al-Kharqi, and Abu 'Abd Allah al-Azdi, companion of Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib. He was a scholar of fundamentals, a teacher of it, and he has works in it, including the book "al-Mustaw'ab fi Usul al-Fiqh", and the book "al-Intisar", which he abridged from the book "al-Intisar" by Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib, and he has "A Treatise on Beliefs". He is among the notables of the second half of the fifth century.' (2) His saying: 'more stuck' (wuhulan) is from al-wahal, which is thin mud. It is said: The beast became stuck (wahilat) in the mud: it fell into it and could not get up.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a critique of those who devote themselves solely to worldly sciences like grammar and arithmetic while neglecting the fundamentals of religion. The author quotes Shaykh Abu al-Qasim 'Abd al-Jalil, who describes such people as arrogant and contemptuous of others. They are unable to answer basic theological questions: the first obligation, when obligation begins, and evidence for their beliefs. They are humbled and silent. The author then provides a biography of the Shaykh and a linguistic note on the word 'wuhulan' (stuck).

Shaykh Abu al-Qasim 'Abd al-Jalil said in his 'Aqida (1): (Know that many people do not occupy themselves except with the science of grammar and arithmetic, and correcting pronunciation and perfecting handwriting, because they hope that this will be for them a commodity and a trade to rely upon and a craft. So you see them dragging their tails out of arrogance, and going about puffed up in their excesses, looking at people with an eye of contempt, and glancing at them with a pupil of belittlement. When it is said to him: What is the first of obligations? And when does obligation become due upon a person? And what is the proof of the correctness of what you hold and the corruption of what you reject? ... he remains more silent than a fish, and more stuck in mud than a bird in a net, and what was great becomes small in his resolve, and what was precious and weighty becomes low in his estimation, and he wears the garment of humility, and clothes himself in a robe of disgrace. Oh what a calamity, how great it is upon him! And what a disaster, how immense it is for him!) End.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is an admonition. Sheikh 'Abd al-Jalil criticizes people who exclusively pursue worldly sciences like grammar (النحو) and arithmetic (الحساب), and the mastery of correct pronunciation and beautiful handwriting. They do this for material gain and social status, and they become arrogant, looking down on others. However, when they are asked fundamental religious questions such as 'What is the first obligation upon a person?', 'When does religious accountability begin?', and 'What is the evidence for what you believe and against what you reject?', they are completely unable to answer. The Sheikh uses vivid similes: they are more silent than a fish, more stuck in mud than a bird in a net. Their pride collapses into humility and disgrace. He concludes by lamenting this as a great calamity and disaster for them. The point is to show the necessity of studying theology (ilm al-kalam) over purely worldly learning.

The Virtue of Proof and the Vice of Taqlid
Ibn Rahhal praises knowledge of evidence and sound reasoning, and condemns blind imitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of proofs and sound reflection is considered the axis of religion, and why blind imitation (taqlid) without proof is condemned in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Knowledge of evidence and sound reasoning is the foundation of true religion, while blind imitation is condemned.

Ibn Rahhal, in his commentary on al-Barawi, opens with a powerful commendation of theological reasoning. He sets the stage for the entire study of kalam by declaring that knowledge of proofs and sound reflection is the very axis of religion. This passage not only praises the path of evidence but also warns against abandoning it for the ease of blind imitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the strong language: 'axis of religion', 'worship of prophets', 'vice of taqlid'.
Pay attention to the transition from praise to condemnation.
2
Identify the two main themes
The passage has two clear parts: first, the praise of knowledge of proofs; second, the condemnation of taqlid. Find where the shift occurs.
The shift happens after 'فلما انقرض الفحول'.
3
Connect to the Quranic proof
Ibn Rahhal supports his condemnation by quoting Quran 43:23. Reflect on why this verse is effective evidence.
The verse shows that following ancestors without proof is blameworthy.
4
Ask yourself the significance
Why is taqlid without proof considered a vice? What does this imply for a student of theology?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالَ ابنُ رحال في شرح البروي (۱) في فضل معرفة الأدلَّةِ وحسن النظر : ( اعلم : أَنَّهُ قطب الدينِ (۲) ، وعبادة الأنبياء والمرسلين ، والأئمَّةِ الراشدين ، وسائرِ علماء المسلمين ، عليها درج السلفُ ، وتبعهم على ذلكَ الخلفُ ، فلما انقرضَ الفحولُ وهلكَتِ الصدور ، وخلفَ مِنْ بعدهم أخلافُ استوعروا طريقهم واستصعبوا سبيلهم ، وأناخوا بفناء الراحاتِ وركبوا مراكب الجهالات . . استوطنوا رذيلة التقليد ؛ وهي مُناخ لكلِّ غبي بليد ، وقد ذم الله قوماً قلدوا آباءهم بغير حُجَّةٍ ، قالوا : ( إِنَّا وَجَدْنَا ءَابَاءَنَا عَلَى أُمَّةٍ وَإِنَّا عَلَى وَاثَرِهِم مُّقْتَدُونَ) [الزخرف : ٢٣) ( انتهى
And Ibn Rahhal said in his commentary on al-Barawi (1) concerning the virtue of knowledge of proofs and sound reflection: (Know: that it is the axis of religion (2), and the worship of the prophets and messengers, and the rightly-guided imams, and all the Muslim scholars. Upon it the predecessors traversed, and the successors followed them in that. When the powerful ones passed away and the leaders died, and there came after them successors who found their path rugged and their way difficult, and they alighted in the courtyard of comforts and rode the mounts of ignorances ... they settled into the vice of taqlid (blind imitation); and it is a stopping place for every stupid, dull person. And God has condemned people who imitated their fathers without proof: they said: 'Indeed, we found our fathers upon a religion, and we are, upon their footsteps, following.' [Zukhruf 43:23]) End.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a commendation of theological reasoning. Ibn Rahhal, in his commentary, explains that knowledge of proofs (معرفة الأدلة) and sound reflection (حسن النظر) is the axis of religion (قطب الدين) and the practice of prophets and the righteous. He then describes how later generations abandoned this path, finding it difficult, and instead took to ease and ignorance. They adopted the vice of taqlid (blind imitation), which he calls a resting place for every stupid and dull person. He supports this with the Quranic verse (43:23) where disbelievers excuse their shirk by saying they follow their fathers. The passage thus establishes the necessity of using evidence and reason in religious matters and condemns uncritical following of tradition without proof.

Key Terms
معرفة الأدلة
حسن النظر
قطب الدين
التقليد
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid (blind imitation) is always acceptable in religious matters.
Correction: The passage shows that taqlid without proof is a vice condemned by God. True religious practice requires knowledge of proofs and sound reflection.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Rahhal say is the axis of religion (قطب الدين)?
A) Fasting
B) Knowledge of proofs and sound reflection
C) Pilgrimage
D) Wealth

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of proofs and sound reflection is the axis of religion, and blind imitation without evidence is a condemned vice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are not the first to find the path of evidence difficult. The passage describes people who turned to comfort and ignorance instead. When you face a complex theological question, do you choose the easy answer from those around you, or do you persevere in learning the proofs yourself? Which specific belief in your life are you holding onto without having examined its evidence?
?
The prophets and the righteous worshipped Allah through knowledge of proofs and sound reflection. As you stand in prayer today, ask yourself: Is my worship built on a foundation of understanding and conviction, or am I merely going through motions inherited from my family? Make a commitment this week to learn the proof for one act of worship you practice.
?
God condemns those who say, 'We found our fathers upon a religion, and we follow in their footsteps.' Identify one religious practice or belief that you accept simply because it is the tradition of your community, without having personally verified its evidence. Are you willing to put it to the test of the Quran and sound reasoning, even if it requires changing a cherished habit?

Blame of Taqlid and Warning Against Its Proponents
Sanusi concludes his criticism of taqlid and prays for guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi condemns blind imitation (taqlīd) and how its proponents deceive people, and you will appreciate the importance of reflection (naẓar) in attaining knowledge of Allah.

Core Idea
Blind imitation cuts off the path to knowing Allah.

In this concluding passage, Sanusi intensifies his critique of taqlīd and those who promote it. He exposes their deceptive tactics and warns that they spiritually impoverish the believers. He then turns to prayer, seeking divine guidance and protection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the shift from criticism to prayer.
2
Identify the two deceptions
The passage mentions two ways the proponents of taqlīd deceive people. Find them and note the contrast.
One claims superior knowledge, the other claims safety.
3
Reflect on the poetic quote
Sanusi quotes a line of poetry. What does it imply about those who forbid reflection?
The metaphor is about calling the dead.
4
Consider the prayer
Sanusi ends with a personal prayer. What does he ask for? How does this relate to the lesson?
He asks for guidance and protection from evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنقتصر على هذا القدر في الإشارة إلى ذم التقليدِ ، وذم مَنْ يحرِّضُ عليهِ ، ويذمُّ النظر في علم التوحيدِ ويُحرِّمُهُ ، ويقطع طريق المعرفة بالله تعالى على عبادِ اللهِ ، ويغرُّهم تارةً بأنَّهم على أحسنِ معرفة ، وتارةً بأنَّ تقليدهم صحيح ليسَ فِيهِ مَتْلَفَةٌ ، والله سبحانَهُ حسيب شياطين الإنس القاطعين على المساكين طرق معايشهم الدينيَّةِ . لَقَدْ أَسْمَعْتَ لَوْ نَادَيْتَ حَيّاً وَلَكِنْ لا حَيَاةَ لِمَنْ تُنَادِي . والله سبحانَهُ أسألُ أنْ يلهمنا رشدَ أنفسنا ، وأنْ يقينا شرَّ أنفسنا وشرَّ كل ذي شرّ ، بفضلِهِ وكرمِهِ ، وصلَّى الله على سيدنا ومولانا محمد نبيه وعبده
And we will limit ourselves to this much in indicating the blame of blind imitation (al-taqlīd), and the blame of those who incite it, and who blame reflection (al-naẓar) in the science of divine unity (ʿilm al-tawḥīd) and forbid it, and cut off the path of knowledge of Allah Most High from His servants, and deceive them sometimes by saying that they are upon the best knowledge, and sometimes by saying that their blind imitation is correct with no destruction in it. And Allah, glorified is He, is the reckoner of the satans among mankind who cut off from the poor the paths of their religious livelihoods. Indeed you would have made hear if you called a living being, but there is no life for the one you call. And Allah, glorified is He, I ask to inspire us with righteousness of ourselves, and to protect us from the evil of ourselves and the evil of every possessor of evil, by His grace and generosity. And may Allah bless our master and patron Muḥammad, His prophet and servant.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a concluding exhortation in which Sanusi summarizes his criticism of blind imitation (taqlīd) and its proponents. He states that he has said enough to indicate the blameworthiness of taqlīd and of those who encourage it, who also disparage and forbid rational reflection (naẓar) in the science of tawḥīd. These people, he says, cut off the path of maʿrifa (knowledge) of Allah from His servants. They deceive the people in two ways: sometimes by claiming that they already possess the best knowledge, and sometimes by saying that their taqlīd is sound and involves no danger (matlafa). Sanusi then invokes Allah as the reckoner against such 'satans among mankind' who deprive the poor of their religious livelihoods. He quotes a line of poetry: 'You would have made hear if you called a living being, but there is no life for the one you call,' implying that those who forbid reflection are spiritually dead and cannot respond. The passage ends with a prayer for divine guidance, protection from evil, and blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad.

Key Terms
تقليد
نظر
معرفة
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that blindly following religious authorities (taqlīd) is always safe and sufficient for attaining true knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi warns that taqlīd without reflection can cut off the path to knowledge of Allah and deceive people into thinking they are on the right path while they are not.

Checkpoint
What do the proponents of taqlīd say to deceive people according to this passage?
A) That reflection is obligatory upon everyone
B) That their taqlīd is correct and contains no danger
C) That knowledge of Allah is impossible
D) That the poor have no religious duties

Key Takeaway
Blind imitation (taqlīd) that rejects reflection (naẓar) is blameworthy and can cut off the path to true knowledge of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage warns of 'satans among mankind' who cut off religious livelihoods. Could you be unintentionally playing that role for someone else by discouraging their questions or deep thinking about faith? How will you change that today?
?
Sanusi prays to be protected from the evil of the self. What specific step will you take this week to ensure your soul does not lead you to prefer comfortable imitation over the hard work of knowing Allah?

Dialectic and Rhetoric: Persuasion and Acceptance
Sanusi defines dialectic and rhetoric and states their purposes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions and purposes of dialectic and rhetoric, and how they differ from demonstration.

Core Idea
Dialectic targets the incapable; rhetoric targets the masses.

Sanusi begins by distinguishing two of the five logical arts: dialectic and rhetoric. Both aim to persuade, but they rely on different premises and address different audiences. Let’s examine his definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on dialectic and rhetoric
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the structure: first dialectic, then rhetoric. Identify the purpose of each.
The purpose of dialectic is given first, then rhetoric.
2
Understand the types of premises
Notice that rhetoric uses accepted or probable premises, illustrated by the example of a thief at night. Dialectic does not specify premises here.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: Why would Sanusi consider these arts less certain than demonstration? The commentary will clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغرضُ مِنَ الجدلِ : إقناع قاصر عنِ البرهان ، أو إلزام الخصم
والخطابة : هي ما تألَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مقبولةٍ مِنْ شخص مُعتقَد فيه ؛ لزهد فيه ونحوِهِ ، أو مُقدِّماتٍ مظنونةٍ ؛ كقولنا : هذا يدور في الليل بالسلاح ، وكلُّ مَنْ يدورُ في الليل بالسلاح فهو لص ؛ فهذا لص .
والغرضُ مِنَ الخَطابةِ : ترغيب السامعين .
والشعرُ : هو ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مُتخيَّلة لترغيب النفس في شيء ، أو تنفيرها عنه ؛ فالأول : كقولِ مَنْ يُرغَبُ في شربِ الخمر : هذه خمرةٌ ، وكلُّ خمرة ياقوتةٌ سيَّالة ؛ فهذهِ ياقوتة سيَّالة ، والثاني : كقولِ مَنْ يُنفِّرُ عن شرب العسل : هذا عسل ، وكلُّ عسل فهو قيء حيوان ؛ فهذا قيء حيوان .
والغرضُ مِنَ الشعرِ : انفعالُ النفسِ ؛ أي : تأثرها وتكيفها بحب شيء أو بغضه .
والمغالطةُ : ما تألَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتِ شبيهة بالحقِّ وليسَتْ بحقِّ ؛ وتُسمَّى سفسطةً ، أو مُقدِّماتِ شبيهة بالمقدِّماتِ المشهورة وليسَتْ بها ؛ وتُسمَّى مشاغبةً ، أو مِنْ مُقدِّماتِ وهميَّةٍ كاذبة
وبه تعلم : أن الوزن الشعري المعروف لا يشترط هنا
مثالُ الأَوَّلِ : قولنا في صورةِ فرس في حائط : هذا فرس ، وكلُّ فرس صمَّالُ ؛ فهذا صهَّالُ
ومثالُ الثاني : قولنا في شخص يَخبِطُ في البحثِ : هذا يُكلَّمُ العلماء بألفاظ العلم حتى يسكتوا ، وكلُّ مَنْ يُكلِّم العلماء بألفاظ العلم فهو عالم ؛ فهذا عالم .
ومثالُ الثالث : قولنا في حَبْلٍ على صورة حيَّةٍ : هذا شكل حيَّةٍ وكلُّ ما كانَ كذلكَ فالحزمُ الفِرارُ منهُ ؛ فهذا الحزمُ الفِرارُ منه والغرضُ مِنَ المغالطة : تغطية الحق ، وتزيين الباطل
والمُعتمد عليهِ مِنْ هذه الصناعات الخمس : البرهان
The purpose of dialectic (jadal) is persuasion of one incapable of demonstration (burhān), or compelling an opponent. Rhetoric (khatāba) is what is composed of premises accepted from a person in whom one has confidence, due to asceticism in him and the like, or probable premises; like our saying: 'This one walks at night with weapons, and whoever walks at night with weapons is a thief; so this is a thief.' The purpose of rhetoric is to persuade the listeners. Poetry (shiʿr) is what is composed of imagined premises to attract the soul to something or to repel it; the first is like the saying of one who encourages drinking wine: 'This is wine, and every wine is a flowing ruby; so this is a flowing ruby.' The second is like the saying of one who discourages drinking honey: 'This is honey, and every honey is animal vomit; so this is animal vomit.' The purpose of poetry is the soul's being affected, i.e., its impression and disposition towards love or hatred of something. Sophistry (mugālaṭa) is what is composed of premises resembling truth but not true; it is called sophistry (sufsaṭa), or premises resembling well-known premises but not well-known; it is called quarrelsomeness (mushāghaba), or from false imaginary premises. By this you know that the well-known poetic meter is not required here. Example of the first: our saying about a picture of a horse on a wall: 'This is a horse, and every horse neighs; so this neighs.' Example of the second: our saying about a person who confuses in discussion: 'This one addresses scholars with terms of knowledge until they become silent, and whoever addresses scholars with terms of knowledge is a scholar; so this is a scholar.' Example of the third: our saying about a rope in the shape of a snake: 'This is the shape of a snake, and everything that is like that, the prudent thing is to flee from it; so the prudent thing is to flee from it.' The purpose of sophistry is to cover truth and adorn falsehood. The relied-upon among these five arts is demonstration (burhān).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the five logical arts used in argumentation, particularly in kalām. The author defines each art and states its purpose. First, dialectic (jadal) is used to persuade someone incapable of following a demonstration or to force an opponent into a contradiction. Second, rhetoric (khatāba) relies on premises accepted on authority or probability, aiming to move an audience. Third, poetry (shiʿr) uses imagined premises to evoke emotional attraction or repulsion; the author notes that conventional poetic meter is not a requirement here. Fourth, sophistry (mugālaṭa) is divided into three types: (a) arguments resembling truth but false (sufsaṭa), (b) arguments resembling well-known premises but not (mushāghaba), and (c) arguments from false imaginary premises. Examples illustrate each: a picture of a horse, a pretender to learning, and a rope mistaken for a snake. The purpose of sophistry is to conceal truth and decorate falsehood. Finally, the author states that among these five, only demonstration (burhān) is the authoritative method for attaining certainty.

Key Terms
جدل
خطابة
إقناع
مقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of dialectic according to this passage?
A) To produce certainty
B) To persuade one incapable of demonstration or compel an opponent
C) To adorn falsehood
D) To affect the soul

Key Takeaway
Dialectic and rhetoric are persuasive arts that do not achieve the certainty of demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a religious lecture, do you accept it because the speaker is respected or because you have verified the evidence? Are you content with rhetoric when you should seek demonstration?
?
Have you ever used rhetoric (appealing to authority or probable reasons) to convince someone of a truth without having certain proof yourself? How does that affect your accountability before Allah?
?
Which of your beliefs about Islam are based on rhetoric (trusted authority) rather than demonstration? Are you willing to examine them and seek certainty, even if it is uncomfortable?

Poetry: Imagined Premises and Emotional Effect
Sanusi defines poetry and gives examples of imagined premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how poetry uses imagined premises to influence the soul toward attraction or repulsion.

Core Idea
Poetry works through imagination, not logic.

Sanusi now turns to poetry, explaining that its premises are imagined rather than real. Its goal is to move the soul emotionally, not to establish truth. Let’s read his definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on poetry
Read the Arabic text. Notice the two examples: one encouraging wine, one discouraging honey.
The examples are analogical but based on imagined comparisons.
2
Identify the purpose
Sanusi states that the purpose is the soul’s being affected – attraction or repulsion.
3
Note the side comment about meter
Later in the text (in the next lesson), he mentions that poetic meter is not required. This is a clarification for the logical definition.
This comment appears in the sophistry section but pertains to poetry.

Sophistry: Deceptive Arguments and the Superiority of Demonstration
Sanusi classifies sophistry into three types and declares demonstration the only reliable art.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of sophistry and why demonstration is the only relied-upon art among the five.

Core Idea
Sophistry deceives; demonstration alone delivers certainty.

In this final part, Sanusi describes sophistry (maghalata) with its three subtypes and provides examples. He concludes by stating that among the five arts, only demonstration (burhan) is trustworthy. Let’s read carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sophistry
Read the Arabic text from 'والغالطة' to the end. Note the three types and their examples.
The text includes a note about poetic meter, which pertains to the previous lesson.
2
Identify the three types
Sophistry uses premises resembling truth (sophism), resembling well-known premises (quarrelsomeness), or false imaginary premises.
3
Study the examples
Each example illustrates one type: horse picture, pretender to learning, rope as snake.
4
Understand the purpose and conclusion
Sophistry aims to cover truth and adorn falsehood. Sanusi ends by affirming that demonstration is the relied-upon art.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والخطابة : هي ما تألَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مقبولةٍ مِنْ شخص مُعتقَد فيه ؛ لزهد فيه ونحوِهِ ، أو مُقدِّماتٍ مظنونةٍ ؛ كقولنا : هذا يدور في الليل بالسلاح ، وكلُّ مَنْ يدورُ في الليل بالسلاح فهو لص ؛ فهذا لص .
والغرضُ مِنَ الخَطابةِ : ترغيب السامعين .
والشعرُ : هو ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مُتخيَّلة لترغيب النفس في شيء ، أو تنفيرها عنه ؛ فالأول : كقولِ مَنْ يُرغَبُ في شربِ الخمر : هذه خمرةٌ ، وكلُّ خمرة ياقوتةٌ سيَّالة ؛ فهذهِ ياقوتة سيَّالة ، والثاني : كقولِ مَنْ يُنفِّرُ عن شرب العسل : هذا عسل ، وكلُّ عسل فهو قيء حيوان ؛ فهذا قيء حيوان .
والغرضُ مِنَ الشعرِ : انفعالُ النفسِ ؛ أي : تأثرها وتكيفها بحب شيء أو بغضه .
والمغالطةُ : ما تألَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتِ شبيهة بالحقِّ وليسَتْ بحقِّ ؛ وتُسمَّى سفسطةً ، أو مُقدِّماتِ شبيهة بالمقدِّماتِ المشهورة وليسَتْ بها ؛ وتُسمَّى مشاغبةً ، أو مِنْ مُقدِّماتِ وهميَّةٍ كاذبة
وبه تعلم : أن الوزن الشعري المعروف لا يشترط هنا
مثالُ الأَوَّلِ : قولنا في صورةِ فرس في حائط : هذا فرس ، وكلُّ فرس صمَّالُ ؛ فهذا صهَّالُ
ومثالُ الثاني : قولنا في شخص يَخبِطُ في البحثِ : هذا يُكلَّمُ العلماء بألفاظ العلم حتى يسكتوا ، وكلُّ مَنْ يُكلِّم العلماء بألفاظ العلم فهو عالم ؛ فهذا عالم .
ومثالُ الثالث : قولنا في حَبْلٍ على صورة حيَّةٍ : هذا شكل حيَّةٍ وكلُّ ما كانَ كذلكَ فالحزمُ الفِرارُ منهُ ؛ فهذا الحزمُ الفِرارُ منه والغرضُ مِنَ المغالطة : تغطية الحق ، وتزيين الباطل
والمُعتمد عليهِ مِنْ هذه الصناعات الخمس : البرهان
The purpose of dialectic (jadal) is persuasion of one incapable of demonstration (burhān), or compelling an opponent. Rhetoric (khatāba) is what is composed of premises accepted from a person in whom one has confidence, due to asceticism in him and the like, or probable premises; like our saying: 'This one walks at night with weapons, and whoever walks at night with weapons is a thief; so this is a thief.' The purpose of rhetoric is to persuade the listeners. Poetry (shiʿr) is what is composed of imagined premises to attract the soul to something or to repel it; the first is like the saying of one who encourages drinking wine: 'This is wine, and every wine is a flowing ruby; so this is a flowing ruby.' The second is like the saying of one who discourages drinking honey: 'This is honey, and every honey is animal vomit; so this is animal vomit.' The purpose of poetry is the soul's being affected, i.e., its impression and disposition towards love or hatred of something. Sophistry (mugālaṭa) is what is composed of premises resembling truth but not true; it is called sophistry (sufsaṭa), or premises resembling well-known premises but not well-known; it is called quarrelsomeness (mushāghaba), or from false imaginary premises. By this you know that the well-known poetic meter is not required here. Example of the first: our saying about a picture of a horse on a wall: 'This is a horse, and every horse neighs; so this neighs.' Example of the second: our saying about a person who confuses in discussion: 'This one addresses scholars with terms of knowledge until they become silent, and whoever addresses scholars with terms of knowledge is a scholar; so this is a scholar.' Example of the third: our saying about a rope in the shape of a snake: 'This is the shape of a snake, and everything that is like that, the prudent thing is to flee from it; so the prudent thing is to flee from it.' The purpose of sophistry is to cover truth and adorn falsehood. The relied-upon among these five arts is demonstration (burhān).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the five logical arts used in argumentation, particularly in kalām. The author defines each art and states its purpose. First, dialectic (jadal) is used to persuade someone incapable of following a demonstration or to force an opponent into a contradiction. Second, rhetoric (khatāba) relies on premises accepted on authority or probability, aiming to move an audience. Third, poetry (shiʿr) uses imagined premises to evoke emotional attraction or repulsion; the author notes that conventional poetic meter is not a requirement here. Fourth, sophistry (mugālaṭa) is divided into three types: (a) arguments resembling truth but false (sufsaṭa), (b) arguments resembling well-known premises but not (mushāghaba), and (c) arguments from false imaginary premises. Examples illustrate each: a picture of a horse, a pretender to learning, and a rope mistaken for a snake. The purpose of sophistry is to conceal truth and decorate falsehood. Finally, the author states that among these five, only demonstration (burhān) is the authoritative method for attaining certainty.

برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think sophistry is a single method; the passage shows it has three distinct types.
Correction: Sophistry includes three subtypes: sophism proper, quarrelsomeness, and imaginary premises.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the five logical arts is the relied-upon one?
A) Dialectic
B) Rhetoric
C) Poetry
D) Demonstration

Key Takeaway
Among all logical arts, only demonstration provides certainty; sophistry deliberately deceives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you were convinced by a smooth talker or a clever story that later turned out to be false. What was the 'premise resembling truth' that deceived you? How will you guard against such sophistry in the future?
?
Sanusi says sophistry aims to 'cover truth and adorn falsehood'. Is there an area of your life where you are covering up a hard truth with comfortable excuses? What would it cost you to face it honestly?
?
The passage declares demonstration as the only relied-upon art. In your pursuit of faith, are you settling for arguments that sound convincing but lack true certainty? What is one belief you hold that you cannot prove with demonstration, and what will you do about it?
